# 太上一乘海空智藏经七宝庄严经

**The Supreme One-Vehicle Scripture of the Ocean of Wisdom Treasury of Emptiness**

**Scripture Title:** *The Supreme One-Vehicle Scripture of the Ocean of Wisdom Treasury of Emptiness* (abbreviated as *Scripture of the Ocean of Emptiness*).

The *Zhenping Lun* by Xuanting of the Tang Dynasty records that Daoists Li Xing of Yizhou and Fang Chang of Lizhou jointly composed the *Scripture of the Ocean of Emptiness* in ten scrolls. According to sources, Li Yuanxing and Fang Huichang were both Daoists during the reign of Emperor Gaozong of the Tang. The base text is from the *Zhengtong Daoist Canon*, *Dongzhen* section, *Benwen* category. Additionally, over ten fragmentary Dunhuang manuscripts exist, including P.2759.

**Table of Contents**
Scroll 1: *Chapter of the Preface*
Scroll 2: *Chapter of Lamentation*
Scroll 3: *Chapter of Dharma Characteristics*
Scroll 4: *Chapter of Universal Teachings*
Scroll 5: *Chapter of Inquiring About Illness*
Scroll 6: *Chapter of Upholding Precepts*
Scroll 7: *Chapter of Equality*
Scroll 8: *Chapter of Offerings*
Scroll 9: *Chapter of Abandonment and Reception*
Scroll 10: *Chapter of Universal Record*

---

**The Supreme One-Vehicle Scripture of the Ocean of Wisdom Treasury of Emptiness, Scroll 1**
*Chapter of the Preface* #1

At that time, the Ocean of Wisdom Treasury of Emptiness beheld the Primordial Heavenly Worthy of Original Beginning traversing other realms, arriving at the wondrous peaks of the Land of Ultimate Bliss. Seated in right mindfulness beneath the Kāñcana grove, he was accompanied by one thousand three hundred disciples, all of whom had attained penetrating insight, comprehending without obstruction. In a single instant, they could reach the Three Realms, perceiving all the myriad phenomena—mountains and rivers, lands, the Six Paths, the Four Modes of Birth, the sentient and insentient—with perfect clarity. The Great Illumination of the Turning Wheel was fully realized in wisdom. Embracing all foreign realms and blending with all beings, they regarded the ten thousand kinds as equal, all utterly empty and quiescent.

Amidst the limpid vastness of the mysterious desert, they abided in profound stillness, contemplating with unwavering focus, dwelling in solemn reflection—thus were all myriad delusions barred from entry. His appellations were the Youth of Supreme Wisdom, the Radiant Wondrous Youth, the Youth of True Samādhi, and the Immortal Minister Youth, with suchlike as his retinue.

At that time, to the west of the land stood a great city named Treasure Assembly, encircling over four hundred *li* in circumference. This city’s adornments were unmatched in the world, surpassing the eight directions in splendor and magnificence. Its terrain was level and even, devoid of hills, pits, perilous mountains, or obstructing forests. Sweet fruits flourished abundantly, the branches of trees grew in perfect pairs, their blossoms and fruits fragrant, luxuriant through all four seasons, resplendent in vibrant hues like a forest of the seven treasures. At the slightest breeze, elegant melodies arose, and those who heard its sound were instantly purified, awakening to the Dao.

Within this city, the populace thrived in abundance, their dwellings connected roof to roof, door to door, with no vacant space between. At all three periods of the day, they expounded the most wondrous principles—namely, that all dharmas of the three times are like sighs, like scoffs, like hisses, like breaths, like calls, like inhalations, all utterly without substance. Such were the countless millions who gathered as his retinue, their voices of exposition resounding through heaven and earth.

To the southwest of the city dwelt a true adept, two thousand years in age, of dignified and peerless countenance, unmatched by any companion. His wealth of treasures enriched the entire city, while his wisdom and spiritual powers moved freely as he willed.

He would always sit in solemn stillness beneath a single tree. When midday arrived, boundless multitudes from all times would bow and present their doubts. Then the true adept would analyze each question in turn, leaving none displeased—they would depart with reverence, their heads bowed in gratitude. Such was his teaching and transformation, accumulated over many years. His propagation of the supreme truth reached everywhere, even liberating all birds and beasts. Having become the object of veneration for all things, the king and his consorts all paid homage and made offerings, his majesty and virtue towering, unmatched by gods or men. The people of the city together named the true adept "Ocean-Empty Wisdom Treasury." The reason for this was that his person was like the sea, his mind like emptiness, his principles embracing all beyond phenomena—thus he was called the Wisdom Treasury.

Upon hearing that the Heavenly Honored One had arrived in this land, his heart swelled with great joy, and he declared: "None in this kingdom can rival me; all their words and discourses fail to stir or inspire. I have long pondered matters that remain unresolved, with nowhere to seek answers, and have often sighed deeply over this. Now I hear that the Heavenly Honored One’s wisdom and virtue are supreme, his spiritual powers uniquely transcendent, his form and bearing radiant, his light and color dazzling. A single word from his mouth subdues all in the assembly; in guiding and receiving sentient beings, he never falters or retreats."

The lands he traversed all benefited thereby. Having heard his renown, I have long wished to pay homage. Today I shall command my chariot and proceed directly to his abode to consult on matters unresolved.

He then instructed his disciples to prepare all carriages, ensuring they were impeccably arrayed, without a trace of shabbiness. "At dawn tomorrow," he said, "I shall go to pay my respects to the Wise One."

Just then, a disciple named Doubt Arisen stepped forward and addressed his master: "Venerable One, why do you entertain such base thoughts? What does this mean—suddenly speaking thus? Your wisdom here is unmatched; not only do the king and his subjects revere you, but even within the Three Realms, your name is extolled. Why debase yourself by seeking out another for the Dharma? How could we bear this humiliation—following you to beg for teachings? What perverse folly! I pray you abandon this journey."

The master replied to Doubt Arisen: "Worthy one, do not cling so. Consider this: if a man stands atop a high terrace and gazes down, he sees the multitudes below, none of whom can reach him. Those multitudes, looking up from below, strain to see the heights, their aspirations cut short. The man on the terrace, believing himself supreme, declares, ‘Here I stand at the utmost peak, unrivaled and unsurpassed.’ Such is his delusion, for above him stretch the Twenty-Eight Heavens, the Three Clarities, and the Jade Capital—boundless and infinite. The multitudes below see only this height; unable to reach it, they claim none surpasses it, yet they too harbor a sense of inferiority. Though I am far removed from such a terrace, it is no different than if we dwelt in separate lands. To reach it would suffice—what more could one desire? Both the self-exalting man on the terrace and the self-deprecating multitudes below err equally. My wisdom surpasses that of the man on the terrace. Why then do you align yourself with the base-minded below? If he is my inferior, my visit to him incurs no loss of reputation; if he surpasses me, my journey brings great gain. Why deem this unreasonable?"

Upon hearing these words, Doubt Arisen’s mind opened, and his understanding deepened. He then prepared offerings for the journey: banners, canopies, palanquins, fragrant flowers, music, and countless wondrous treasures. At midday, they mounted their carriages and set forth. Their retinue of disciples numbered fifty thousand, all possessed of great wisdom, deeply versed in the sources of the Dharma, and lucid in the nature of emptiness—such as the emptiness that is not empty, the absence of self in phenomena, the non-abiding nature of things, and the unreality of forms.

Each disciple also prepared various treasures and offerings, accompanying their master as they journeyed together. Arriving at the mountain’s edge, they halted their carriages and rested, strolling leisurely through the marshes.

From the assembly, a disciple named Great Wisdom stepped forward, knelt, and addressed his master: "The path has been long, and we have traversed mountains and forests. Are you not weary? Today, I wish to pose a question; may the Great Master deign to hear my humble words."

The master replied: "Ask as you will, and I shall listen attentively."

Great Wisdom then said: "Great Master, your virtue is unfathomable to gods and men alike; all things revere you as they do their parents. The hearts of the multitude turn to you as their great refuge. Your discourses and teachings serve as a model for others; your words are weighty and not to be spoken lightly. If we meet this person, I first beg to engage him in debate. If I hold my own, you may remain silent, and your esteemed reputation will stand unchallenged, sparing you the effort of exchange. Is this not sound?"

The master answered: "What you say is not amiss."

Entering the mountains, they saw the Heavenly Honored One from afar—his radiance towering like the Diamond King, his brilliance illuminating the mountains as the rising sun. His majestic light and dignified bearing struck awe into all who beheld him. The master and his disciples were all astounded: some concealed themselves, some rejoiced, some trembled in fear, some blushed with shame, their hearts stirred in myriad ways, their intentions shifting.

Having arrived, they dismounted from their carriages and took their seats in order. Each was filled with trepidation, as if something had been lost. After a long while, the Heavenly Honored One’s countenance brightened, and he appeared to smile. His retinue then relaxed slightly, their breath steadying.

The disciple Great Wisdom rose and approached the Heavenly Honored One, standing before him to pay respects and inquire after his well-being: "Have your wanderings in the mountains caused you any harm?"

After the Heavenly Honored One had responded, Great Wisdom spoke again: "I have heard that your spiritual powers are sovereign, your eloquence unobstructed, and that you fully comprehend the affairs of beings across countless kalpas. Moreover, your wisdom perceives the desires and aspirations of all hearts without hindrance—hence you are called the Heavenly Honored One. Today, we wish to consult you on certain matters; may you expound broadly upon them."

The Heavenly Honored One then replied to Great Wisdom: "Excellent, excellent, good man, you speak rightly."

Great Wisdom then asked the Heavenly Honored One: "Among all dharmas, which is the foremost for attaining the fruit of the Way? Which is the foremost for attaining wisdom? Which is the foremost for attaining spiritual powers? Which is the foremost for attaining quiescence, the unconditioned, the unobstructed, the supreme and foremost? Among these four paths, how do their causes differ in cultivation? How do their attainments vary, and their accomplishments diverge? I pray you clarify and elucidate this, that we and the people of this land may cultivate accordingly."

The Heavenly Honored One replied to Great Wisdom: "Excellent is this question, excellent is this question. One who asks thus will surely serve as a great bridge, a great vessel, a great treasure chariot for all the world..."

Those capable of elevating the world to the rank of *rushi* (perfected beings), I shall now distinguish and explain for you, that those who have not yet heard may all come to hear and know, and thus attain liberation. Listen carefully and discern well.

Good son, the conduct of people in the Three Realms varies—some are restless, some tranquil; some foolish, some wise. Yet the foolish are not dull-witted by nature, nor are the wise endowed with sublime, responsive wisdom. Restlessness does not persist in constant motion, nor does tranquility persist in perpetual stillness. Judging by what prevails, there are degrees of superiority and inferiority. From these prevailing tendencies, names arise: the restless are called "restless people," the tranquil "tranquil people," the wise "wise people," and the foolish "foolish people."

Among these four dispositions, one may be wise yet restless, or foolish yet tranquil. Those who are wise yet restless cannot attain the fruit of the Way. Why is this so? Their wisdom is not yet complete, and restlessness compounds their agitation. With such limited wisdom, they pursue many paths. Their minds unsettled, good and evil become indistinguishable, and thus they fail to attain the Way.

What is the fruit of the Way? There are five kinds: the fruit of earthly immortals, the fruit of flying immortals, the fruit of self-mastery, the fruit of the untainted, and the fruit of the unconditioned. Though the intentions behind these five fruits differ, they all converge at a single source. To cultivate these fruits, one must employ a tranquil mind—tranquility and single-minded focus ensure success in practice.

What are these practices? The five fruits correspond to five endeavors, and the cultivator must understand their causes, conditions, and the intentions that guide them.

If one practices alchemical ingestion, fasting, refining the essence of the Dao’s flavor, yearning for mountain forests, concealing one’s form in seclusion, consuming mist and absorbing *qi*, forsaking rich delicacies, and abandoning fatty foods—lightening the body, strengthening its vigor, and escaping old age and death—this is called the *earthly immortal*.

If one is born into nobility or wealth, dwells in splendor, suffers no illness or distress, observes long fasts with vegetarian fare, and renounces lust—this, too, is called the *earthly immortal*.

If one is born with robust *qi* and strength, directs the mind to the root of the Dao, refrains from consuming living beings, practices the Dharma of compassion, and attains a lifespan of one hundred and twenty years—this is called the *earthly immortal*.

Those who strive for the fruit of earthly immortals must accumulate learning and maintain unwavering resolve. If one abandons alchemical practices midway, illness follows; if one abandons seclusion in forests and marshes, peril ensues. A mind burdened by excessive concerns cannot remain single-minded; a mind adrift cannot achieve deep tranquility. A mind lacking unity and thoughts lacking stillness cannot long dwell in renowned mountains, sipping from pine streams. Thus, earthly immortality demands tranquil focus. Even the foolish and unlearned, if they cultivate the methods described above without studying scriptures, may attain the fruit of earthly immortals.

Good son, if one practices the ten virtuous deeds, upholds precepts without transgression, aligns body and mind, fasts and abstains from grains, guards the six senses, avoids defilement, conducts oneself with integrity, maintains inner and outer consistency, traverses sacred peaks, and consumes cloud and mist—cultivating such arts—this is called the *flying immortal*.

By what mental effort is the fruit of flying immortals attained? It, too, requires a tranquil mind, steadfast and unshaken. Though one may not widely study the wondrous tones of the *Great Cavern*, a heart of unyielding purity—like a lapis lazuli bead, radiant within and without—can, through such cultivation, attain the fruit of flying immortals.

Good son, if one thoroughly comprehends precepts and observes manifold decorum, aligning body and mind with hidden standards, revered by gods and humans who offer homage without exception; if one wields spiritual powers and transformations, manifesting without limitation; and if one’s will and intention achieve all they seek—this is the path to the fruit of self-mastery.

Without consuming elixirs or herbs, one attains natural satiety. Should one wish to eat, sustenance arrives at the mere thought. In this world, one’s form and thought become autonomous, roaming mountains and marshes without fear, preserving long years free from illness and distress—this is called *self-mastery*.

By what mental effort is the fruit of self-mastery attained? One must cultivate the work of wisdom, also requiring a tranquil mind. Though the body may stir in response, the heart remains steadfast as a mountain, unshaken as the earth. Though one may not fully grasp the supreme methods of wisdom, through cultivating its causes one never grows weary, practicing spiritual penetrations and refining contemplative focus. Within this refined concentration, one can divide the body and respond to boundless calls. One who thoroughly comprehends these arts thereby attains the fruit of self-mastery.

Good son, if one fully illuminates and penetrates all dharmas, discerning the coarse and fine movements of others’ minds without error; if one can perceive the karmic conditions of all gods—their virtuous and unwholesome deeds, signs of decline and transformation, all actions and conduct—and thereby benefit immeasurable multitudes, instantly responding to those ready for liberation and delivering them all; if one constantly cultivates precepts and sublime scriptures, mastering all foundations of the Dao without omission; if one enters every gate of spiritual penetrations and every realm of wisdom, as well as the threshold between emptiness and existence; if one receives and upholds all scriptures of the Supreme Great Dao, free from afflictions—then all afflictions, such as those of wrong views, wrong faith, greed, hatred, and delusion, are wholly abandoned, with no lingering attachment.

There are five kinds of attachment: the eye’s attachment to forms, the ear’s to sounds, the tongue’s to sweetness, the nose’s to fragrances, and the body’s to smoothness. When all such attachments are eradicated without remainder, the six senses become pure. These six senses—eye, ear, nose, tongue, body, and mind—when their faculties are clear, determine all that is received and contemplated as the profound scriptures of the Three Caverns.

There are also external fetters: the three fetters. The first is the fetter of obscurity when entering the realm of dharma. The second is the fetter of obstruction when following dharma companions. The third is the fetter of incompletion in all vows and practices. When these fetters are forever severed without residue, the mind becomes luminously open—this is the fruit of the *untainted*.

By what mental effort is the fruit of the untainted attained? One must cultivate wisdom. Though one may not fully attain all spiritual penetrations, one diligently practices the boundless gates of the Supreme’s immeasurable wisdom, refining contemplative focus and attaining clear illumination. With eloquence in expounding the Dharma, one liberates sentient beings, never regressing in this life or the future, gradually ascending the steps to the supreme Dao, which is no longer distant. Through such cultivation, one attains the fruit of the untainted path.

Good son, the fruit of *non-action* is the entry into the supreme gate of quiescence. Why is this so? Quiescence is non-action, and non-action is quiescence. What is quiescence? It is neither birth nor death, thus it abides eternally; neither destruction nor change, thus it responds to transformation. Non-action is action, and action is non-action; through non-action, action arises, and through action, one enters non-action. Quiescence is responsiveness, and responsiveness is quiescence; through quiescence, responsiveness arises, and through responsiveness, one returns to quiescence. Quiescence is the source of wondrous existence—though spoken of as "existence," it cannot be equated with nothingness. Since it is neither nothingness nor existence, it is called non-action. The discourse on non-action transcends all realms, hence it is called non-action.

By what mental effort is the fruit of non-action attained? One must perfect all varieties of wisdom and spiritual penetrations, fulfill all practices, and surpass gods and humans. It is neither existence nor non-existence, nor is it neither-existence nor neither-non-existence. Because nothing is perceived, it is deemed non-existence; because it responds, it is deemed existence. This is non-action—the supreme fruit.

Furthermore, good son, O Great Wisdom, having attained the root of great wisdom, one cultivates the three characteristics: the characteristic of existence, the characteristic of non-existence, and the characteristic of neither existence nor non-existence. By contemplating these characteristics, one enters the source of wisdom. Yet the observation of these three characteristics diverges.

Some beings contemplate from existence to enter non-existence. Some beings contemplate from non-existence to enter existence. Some beings, with resolute spirit, transcend these two characteristics and perceive the nature of existence and non-existence.

Good son, if there are beings who, as students of emptiness, constantly direct their minds from existence to observe non-existence, asserting, "I see all things—though they appear now, they must ultimately return to nothingness. Know that all is fundamentally void, without true substance, devoid of profound wisdom, devoid of contemplative wisdom, devoid of responsive wisdom." Know that such a person holds the view of annihilation, which, though resembling wisdom, deviates from its essence.

Good son, if there are beings who constantly direct their minds from non-existence to observe existence, asserting, "All things in this world arise from nothingness. Originally, they did not exist, yet now they do—by what cause do they arise? They must arise from nothingness. Thus, within nothingness, all things exist, and because they exist, all nothingness ceases to be." Know that such a person holds a narrow view, which, though resembling wisdom, loses its essence.

Good son, if there are beings who constantly direct their minds to contemplate existence and non-existence from the perspective of neither existence nor non-existence, asserting, "All beings in this world are neither existent nor non-existent. Why? If they are said to exist, they ultimately return to nothingness; if they are said to be nothing, they are presently seen to exist. If this is so, then their nature is indeterminate. Because their karma is indeterminate, they cannot be liberated."

The rewards of humans and heavens—know that the six destinies arise with form and perish with form. If one holds this view, it is a partial seeing, not true wisdom. These three views all lose the root of wisdom.

Good son, if there is one who contemplates the Three Realms with such a mind, observing all as nothingness—this is desolation, incapable of generating things, utterly unmoving, unable to respond. Good son, it is like a great mountain that well stirs the clouds, and the clouds bring forth rain. From such examples, know decisively that from existence arises existence, and from nothingness arises nothingness.

Again, observing existence clings to form and substance; this form and substance are truly existent. Apart from this form and substance, there is no place to name existence. Further observing neither existence nor non-existence—good son, it is like the sound of wind, its resonance pervading everywhere. Such examples show that the substance of wind is not nothing, yet it cannot be grasped. If one claims it truly exists, it is not obstructive. Echoes are likewise. Know that such examples are neither existent nor non-existent. If a learner holds this view, they too lose the root of wisdom.

Good son, if there is one who observes the three characteristics with four kinds of intention—subtle non-existence, subtle existence, coarse non-existence, and coarse existence—understanding these four intentions, one naturally sees neither existence nor non-existence. What is subtle non-existence? It is the nature of the Dao. For what reason is the principle of Dao-nature called subtle non-existence? Because it is profoundly quiescent and responsive. If one abides in the realm of profound quiescence and observes all existences, one sees no characteristics. If one abides in the realm of responsiveness and observes all existences, one sees characteristics.

Good son, if one claims the Dao-nature is entirely non-existent, it still responds; if one claims the Dao-nature is entirely existent, it is truly tranquil. Therefore, know that the existence of Dao-nature is not worldly existence, and the non-existence of Dao-nature is not worldly non-existence. This is called subtle non-existence. For what reason does one observe subtle existence? It is responsiveness, the beginning of the Dharma-body, flourishing and arising, transcending the Three Realms. Though it has substance, it is not like the ordinary. For this reason, it is called subtle existence.

Good son, for what reason does one observe coarse non-existence? Coarse non-existence is simply nothingness—without response, without correspondence, an empty and desolate place, utterly void. This is called coarse non-existence. Good son, for what reason does one observe coarse existence? Coarse existence is obstructive substance. Because it is obstructive, it is called coarse existence.

Good son, if one can thoroughly understand these four intentions and discern their essence and characteristics, know that this person has come to understand neither existence nor non-existence. For what reason is this known? By tracing the Dao-nature, its meaning can be sought. If a learner practices observing the three characteristics and comprehends these four intentions, they enter the treasure city of wisdom, wander the chamber of wisdom, sit upon the seat of wisdom, and feast on the food of wisdom—this is obtaining the root of the mechanism of wisdom.

Furthermore, good son, excellent indeed, Great Wisdom, you have attained the power of spiritual penetrations through practicing the meaning of subtle contemplation, profound and vast, difficult to debate. For this reason, it is called subtle contemplation. All beings attain the power of spiritual penetrations through nothing other than this. If one attains great samadhi and wields this spiritual power, it is like the wind fanning a fire—within a single thought, all ceases together. Know that subtle contemplation generates various wisdoms of spiritual penetrations.

There are two kinds of spiritual penetrations. What are the two? The first is spontaneously possessing spiritual penetrations, clearly illuminating all, able to know and see, responding to conditions as they arise, not requiring entry into samadhi, naturally complete. In an instant, it generates countless transformations, freely following the mind, never contrary to intention. Such spiritual penetrations are the supreme power. The second kind of spiritual penetration arises from subtle contemplation and exists through samadhi. Without entering samadhi, it cannot arise, manifesting various transformations—this is a secondary power. Yet both these penetrations rely on subtle contemplation to be complete. With long accumulation and practice, one naturally becomes effortlessly and spontaneously transformative.

If a learner seeks spiritual penetrations, they should rightly contemplate the Dao of subtle contemplation, which is the source of spiritual transformations.

Furthermore, good son, excellent indeed, Great Wisdom, seeking quiescent extinction, non-action, and unimpededness—all arise from perfecting various wholesome thoughts and actions, possessing majestic spiritual power, accomplishing boundless merits and virtues. Cultivating boundless practices, the root of all Dao, spiritual power and freedom, penetrating transformations, the essence and characteristics are subtle and wondrous, like pure vaidūrya. All gods and humans, when moved, must respond. Liberating and delivering the four kinds of birth, none are not freed. This is the root of the Dao, the source of wisdom, quiescent extinction, non-action, and unimpededness.

For what reason is it called quiescent extinction? Good son, quiescent extinction is not empty void. Because it is empty and void, it is unstained by the Three Realms, its conduct transcending the mundane. All dharmas cannot disturb it—this is quiescent extinction. Good son, non-action means all myriad things cannot act upon it as a moving function. Its appearance is clear and desolate, its substance profoundly mysterious—this is non-action. Good son, unimpededness means all myriad things cannot obstruct it. Its responsiveness is subtle, its power all-pervading—this is unimpededness. Among these four paths, quiescent extinction is supreme. Therefore, learners should cultivate such various merits and virtues.

Then, Great Wisdom, having heard these words, generated great reverence and admiration, his heart and words in accord: "Ah! Great Virtue, your eloquence is penetrating and free, unimpeded, able to open and develop my wisdom—truly delightful!" He further addressed him: "Great Virtue, Heavenly Honored One, with such majestic spiritual power, attaining fearlessness, possessing great spiritual penetrations, able to deliver ordinary and worthy ones into the right path, traversing the Three Realms, supreme and unmatched."

Today, filled with reverence, I rank you with my teacher." Thereupon, he untied the precious guiding pearl necklace from his upper robe and offered it to the Heavenly Honored One, saying, "Great Virtue, I pray you accept my offering as a token of my sincere devotion."

At that time, the Heavenly Honored One replied to Great Wisdom, "I now dwell in these deep mountains and remote valleys. Such precious and resplendent adornments are unnecessary. To accept them in vain would be wasteful."

Great Wisdom spoke again, "The sage appears in the world, his heart free from vanity or superficiality. For what profound reason does he manifest this body? Why remain hidden in these secluded valleys? I now wish to invite you to journey through this troubled land, which is why I offer this robe."

Then, the Heavenly Honored One silently declined, and in that very moment, transformed the offering into thirty-six thousand white jade guiding necklaces with azure pearls, thirty-six thousand golden guiding necklaces with crimson pearls, thirty-six thousand lapis lazuli guiding necklaces with white pearls, thirty-six thousand agate guiding necklaces with black pearls, and thirty-six thousand amber guiding necklaces with yellow pearls. Such guiding necklaces filled the space beneath his seat, beyond count. He then addressed Great Wisdom, "Do you see among these the multitude of great guiding pearls and necklaces?"

Great Wisdom replied, "Heavenly Honored One, I see them clearly. How is it that such treasures and guiding necklaces have arisen here? Ah! What wondrous spiritual power has brought forth this abundance! How marvelous, Heavenly Honored One, that such auspicious signs have manifested!"

At that time, the Heavenly Honored One answered Great Wisdom, "I appear in the world to serve as the great guiding master for all beings in darkness, decisively leading countless beings out of the sea of birth and death. How then could such precious guiding treasures be considered strange?" Immediately, with his spiritual power, he gathered all the guiding treasures into one. This single guiding treasure filled the entire void, its luminous radiance illuminating all, beyond description.

The assembly of Ocean-void Wisdom Treasury and his retinue all bowed in reverence, none daring to speak. Then, from among the crowd, a disciple named Foremost Foot stepped forward, prostrated before his teacher, and said, "Great Teacher, I have followed you for over a hundred years, humbly receiving your kindness, ascending the hall and entering the inner chamber. Yet never have I witnessed such extraordinary spiritual powers and auspicious signs. Why then does this great virtue suddenly leap in praise? I pray, Great Teacher, exert your wisdom and manifest wondrous signs, that all in this assembly may see and extol them."

At that time, Ocean-void Wisdom Treasury replied to Foremost Foot, "Do not be arrogant or seek superiority. Why? The Heavenly Honored One is majestic, his radiant signs peerless—how could worldly beings compare or equal him? I may yet consult him on my doubts to resolve them. How could I lightly boast of my meager spiritual powers? Say no more."

Then, from the assembly, another named Wondrous Thought stepped forward, joined his palms, bowed at the Heavenly Honored One’s feet, and asked, "Though I am foolish and base, I wish to consult you. I pray, Great Virtue, have compassion and grant your teaching."

The Heavenly Honored One replied to Wondrous Thought, "Excellent, excellent! Ask whatever you wish. I shall explain each matter clearly, that you may fully understand."

Wondrous Thought said, "Great Virtue and Sage Teacher, by what causes and conditions do all things come to be born and die? Why are there the foolish and the wise, those of great power, those of great beauty, the noble and the base, those without dignity, those respected by others, and those held in contempt? Such variations are endless. Are these the fruits of planted causes? Or are they the karmic retributions of cultivated practices? When I reflect, it is difficult to discern clearly. I pray you have compassion and grant a merciful answer."

At that time, the Heavenly Honored One replied to Wondrous Thought, "Excellent, excellent, good son! A great and virtuous scholar indeed, to raise such a profound question. I shall now explain each point clearly, leaving no doubts.

"Wondrous Thought, know this: It is like a person speaking into the empty void, their voice echoing in the valley, returning without deviation. If coarse words are spoken, the echo returns coarse; if kind words are spoken, the echo returns kind; if singing, the echo returns singing; if weeping, the echo returns weeping; if loud, the echo returns loud; if soft, the echo returns soft; if moderate, the echo returns moderate. If calling out, in countless ways, the voice responds in kind, echoing without fail, in thousands upon thousands of instances, never erring.

"Good son, the various good and evil deeds of people in this world are likewise—all arise from the actions of past lives, bearing their fruits. This is no different from the echo following the voice. If in a past life one cultivated a heart of compassion, saving and delivering all beings, in this life they gain great power and virtue, respected by all. If one was deceitful and venomous, lacking compassion, in this life they lack dignity and are not loved by others.

"If in a past life one made offerings to the Three Jewels, cultivated generosity and precepts, diligence, patience, contemplation, and wisdom—such practices—then in this life they gain the power of wholesome karma, spiritual wisdom, fearlessness, and all good roots.

"Good son, if in a past life one universally aided beings, they gain the reward of long life without illness. If in a past life one harmed beings, they gain the retribution of sickness, suffering, and short life. Such karmic fruits are countless, beyond enumeration. All arise from the causes of past lives, bearing these retributions, just as the echo follows the voice, without difference.

"Furthermore, Wondrous Thought, it is like a shadow, which arises from form. If the form is long, the shadow is long; if short, the shadow is short; if large, the shadow is large; if small, the shadow is small. The shadow cannot depart from the form..."

"The size of the form cannot conceal the shadow. All worldly beings experience differing karmic fruits in just this way. Good son, if in a past life one planted small causes, the retribution is small; if large, the retribution is large. Like compass and square, like inked line and marking cord, they correspond precisely, without the slightest deviation. Wondrous Thought, you should understand that the arising of good and evil is like echo and shadow, never departing from form and sound."

Then, Wondrous Thought, hearing these words, conceived a doubt. Adjusting his robe and headdress, he composed his mind and focused his thoughts, then addressed the Heavenly Venerable once more: "Great Virtuous and Sacred Master, the echo responds to the voice—this is indeed as you have so wondrously explained. Yet if one utters a small sound in a deep mountain or secluded valley, where forests and barriers layer thickly, the echo returns greatly amplified. If one utters a great sound in a small chamber or vessel, the echo returns diminished. Though the sound itself is no different, why does the response vary? Moreover, in an open field, though one may emit a sound, there is no echo at all. Why, in such places, does a mighty sound go forth yet find no response? If we extend this analogy, we must conclude that whether the cause be sinful or meritorious, they are equal and without difference—yet some yield great fruit, some small fruit, and some no fruit at all, just as with the emission of sound. At the moment of emission, whether great or small, the sound is the same, yet the echoing response differs according to the place. Again, like form and shadow: when the form meets slanted light, its shadow lengthens; when it meets direct light, its shadow shortens; when it meets no light, its shadow vanishes. Though the form itself does not change, the shadow varies in length and presence. If we extend this analogy, whether the cause be meritorious or sinful, they are equal and without difference—yet some yield long-lasting fruit, some short-lived fruit, and some no fruit at all. Great Virtue, your comparison to echoes and shadows does not accord with principle. If it were not so, it would not be a true analogy. The assembly is perplexed; we humbly beseech your explanation."

Then, the Heavenly Venerable, his mind joyful and at ease, replied to Wondrous Thought: "Excellent is this question! It gives rise to the doubts of the multitude, and its skillful benefit cannot be measured. Now listen carefully and ponder deeply. You ask me why, though the actions are no different, the retribution varies. The principle is indeed as you have said—just as you have said. Why is this so?"

If there are worldly people who take lives, the act of taking a life is no different—yet if they kill a true practitioner, a virtuous person, a Daoist, or one of great virtue, the sin is profound and weighty. Why is this so? Such persons serve as teachers and models for others, benefiting all beings, liberating them from the suffering of birth and death. Without them, all beings would remain trapped in the afflictions of the three evil destinies and the eight difficulties. To take such a life is to sever the roots of goodness for all beings. Thus, the sin is multiplied manyfold. If one kills those of the five rebellious acts, the wicked, or the malevolent—such as these—the sin lies only in the taking of life, not in the harm to goodness. In this case, the karmic retribution is lighter and lesser. Though the act of taking life follows these two paths, identical in form, the retribution differs. It is like the emission of sound: though the sound is the same, the echo varies in size depending on where it resounds.

Again, consider almsgiving: all acts of generosity and offering are meritorious deeds, equal and without difference. Yet if one gives to a true practitioner, a virtuous person, a Daoist, or one of great virtue, the karmic reward is vast and weighty. Why is this so? Such persons propagate teachings and transform the world, delivering beings and benefiting all things. To give to them is to provide the bridge and beam of the Dharma for all beings, the path to the Dao. Know then that the blessings gained are boundless. If one gives to the wicked, those of the four grave offenses, the four afflictions, or the five rebellious acts, though the act of giving is the same, the reward is uncertain. If one gives with a heart of compassion, the merit gained still does not equal that of giving to the virtuous. If one provides for all evil persons with a mind of generosity, the sin incurred is instead great and unlike the former. Why is this so? Because one nourishes and sustains the wicked, thereby harming all things—such giving is no different from harming all things. Thus, though the act of giving is not distinct, the retribution differs. Good man, it is like the emission of sound: though the sound is the same, the echo varies in size depending on where it resounds.

Again, consider the taking of life with an indeterminate mind. If the killer bears no resentment, feels no joy, and simply takes the life without generating either good or ill will in the process, the sin incurred is slightly lighter. Why is this so? Because the heart holds no malice.

Just as a sound emitted in empty space is not without an echo, yet its resonance is faint and inaudible to the ear, so too do beings who commit offenses with an indeterminate mind not escape retribution—though their sin does not become manifest.

Again, consider the act of giving with an indeterminate mind. If, when bestowing alms, one does not arouse a mind of vast relief, nor a mind of deliverance, nor a mind of compassion, nor a mind of joy, nor a mind of envy, nor a mind of surpassing others, nor a mind of boasting, nor a mind of gratitude, nor a mind of stinginess—if one’s giving is like casting dung into the great sea—then know that such a person gains but meager merit. Why is this so? Because there is no good intention. Just as a sound emitted in empty space is not without an echo, yet its resonance is faint and does not reach the ear, so too does one who performs good deeds with an indeterminate mind not fail to gain merit—though that merit does not become manifest. For this reason, what you have said is not mistaken.

Upon hearing this, Miaosi was suddenly enlightened, his mind free of doubt and entanglement. He attained understanding, returned to his seat, and rejoiced with exultation.

At that moment, another in the assembly, named Zuisheng, arose—majestic in bearing, towering above the crowd, foremost disciple of Haikong Zhizang, having mastered the Ten Transformations: the Transformation of No Sorrow, the Transformation of Pure Mind, the Transformation of Dispelling Obstruction, the Transformation of Penetrating the Classics, the Transformation of Attaining Understanding, the Transformation of Clear Vision, the Transformation of Skilled Means, the Transformation of Comprehending Conditions, the Transformation of Great Illumination, and the Transformation of Perfection. He possessed supernatural powers: the eye, ear, nose, tongue, body, and hand. He was endowed with eloquence: the Eloquence of Understanding Intent, the Eloquence of Resolving Afflictions, the Eloquence of Severing Anguish, the Eloquence of Discerning Marks, and the Eloquence of Probing the Profound. Possessing the highest wisdom, he was revered as the crown of the nation.

Rising from his seat, he approached the Heavenly Venerable, paid homage, stood upright with reverence, and fixed his gaze unwaveringly. With single-minded devotion, he addressed the Heavenly Venerable: "For many years, I have diligently studied and accumulated merit, concentrating my thoughts to comprehend the profound Dao. Though I have had questions, I dared not speak first. Today, seeing how Great Wisdom and Miaosi posed their doubts, and how the Heavenly Venerable, in your compassion, bestowed your discerning answers—clarifying principles so that all who heard immediately understood without further obstruction—I am filled with boundless joy. It is like one who, having suffered a grave illness, suddenly recovers as if restored to health. My joy is likewise. Or like one who, having long been parted from parents, unexpectedly encounters them again. My joy is likewise. Or like one who, after days of famine, suddenly comes upon a feast of delicacies. My joy is likewise.

"Yet, Heavenly Venerable, I still harbor a doubt. It is like a man afflicted with a grievous illness, lying upon his sickbed, unable to eat or drink, his breath and life nearly spent. Suddenly, from the empty sky, a vessel brimming with sweet dew medicine appears at his head. The man is startled, suspecting it to be a ghostly poison or a demonic calamity. Or perhaps he wonders if it is a gift from the gods. Hesitating to drink, he wavers—until a benevolent spirit speaks from the void, declaring it a wondrous medicine. Only then does he rejoice, take the medicine, and drink, whereupon his illness vanishes.

"So too am I. Though my doubts have long lingered, upon hearing the Heavenly Venerable’s teaching, I have not yet fully understood. Discerning its nature, my heart remains hesitant, and I dare not accept it outright. If I were to hear further clarification, my mind would be thoroughly enlightened, clear and free of doubt.

"Or consider one who wishes to journey to a distant land but is separated by a great river. Gazing longingly at the far shore, which seems immeasurably distant, he is filled with sorrow and distress, unable to restrain himself. Then, the Benevolent Spirit King, appearing on the riverbank, manifests a boat fully equipped with oars and poles, lacking nothing. A voice from within calls out, offering passage. The man is startled: 'I have waited here so long, searching in vain for a boat—how could one suddenly appear? Surely this is a demon luring me into the water, or some illusory trick that will later harm me. Better to die here than to be deceived and perish in regret.' At that moment, the Benevolent Spirit King transforms into a flying immortal and, amidst the clouds, cries out: 'This is a benevolent spirit seeking to save your life. Cross without fear.' Hearing this, the man rejoices immeasurably, his heart at ease. He boards the boat and safely reaches the far shore.

"So too am I today. Having heard the Heavenly Venerable expound the supreme and wondrous Dharma, I cannot yet fully comprehend it, and my heart remains hesitant. If I were to hear further clarification, I would be free of doubt and regret.

"Heavenly Venerable, Great Sage, if you know the doubt in my heart, I beg you to dispel it, that I may be at peace, free of entanglement."

Then the Heavenly Venerable addressed Zuisheng: "Excellent, excellent, good man. I now know the doubt in your heart. You wonder: 'All beings receive their bodily form due to past karma. Their actions—whether good or evil—and the conditions of their karma are all established by prior causes. It is like form and sound producing echoes. Without form or sound, there would be no echo. Yet if, as the Heavenly Venerable has said today, all beings at the inception of consciousness have no good or evil, how then do they come to differ in goodness and evil, beauty and ugliness, nobility and baseness?' Is this the doubt that binds your heart?"

When Zuisheng heard these words, he was struck with awe, his heart trembling, unable to speak for a long while. Then the Heavenly Venerable asked: "Is this not the doubt in your heart?"

Zuisheng replied: "Great Sage, Heavenly Venerable, only today have I realized that in this world there exists a Venerable One of great wisdom, a Great Illuminator. I rejoice that this world has gained the Supreme Teacher, the Great Guide, the Great Vessel, the Great Eye of Wisdom, the Great Dharma City."

All the world shall now be delivered from fear, from the five destinies, from craving and desire, from sorrow and lamentation, from the three obstructions, from defilement and impurity, from all karmic entanglements. My joy today knows no bounds. Until now, I have indeed harbored such doubts. I pray the Heavenly Venerable will expound and clarify. Once my doubts are dispelled, I shall not only find liberation myself but also bring benefit to the heavenly and human multitudes of the present and future.

Then the Heavenly Venerable proclaimed in a loud voice: "All who have come here today, give ear with single-minded attention to what I shall say." Thus he called three times: "All who have come here today, give ear with single-minded attention to what I shall say. Let no slackness arise, nor let your thoughts wander to other matters."

"Here in this assembly sits a great perfected one named Zuisheng, whose heart is bound by doubt. As I have said, I shall now analyze the principle for him with utmost clarity. Therefore, all of you should heed these words well, that you may gain immeasurable and inconceivable blessings."

"Zuisheng, you must understand: the karmic nature of all beings in the six destinies and four modes of birth, at the inception of consciousness, is purely good, undefiled, and singular, of one body with the Dao, moving in accord with the Dao. In their actions—whether walking, standing, rising, or reclining; speaking, silent, eating, or resting—they all conform to the true principle. Just as a fish, at its first birth, naturally takes to rivers and lakes without need of instruction, or as jade is inherently white, indigo inherently blue, fire inherently hot, and water inherently cold—these qualities are not acquired through learning but are naturally so by principle—so too, at the inception of consciousness, all beings are endowed with the natural Dao-nature, spontaneously in accord with it, without any distinction."

"How then do they come to generate the various evil conditions and suffer the retributions of hell, hungry ghosts, and beasts? You must know that all these arise from the six roots. What are the six roots? The eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, and mind."

"The eye-root, through conditions, becomes attached to forms and contacts. The characteristics of form cling and entangle, their principles manifold, and all manner of forms enter the eye-root. From this condition, the eye-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"The ear-root, through conditions, is likewise attached to sounds and contacts. The characteristics of sound cling and entangle, their principles manifold, and all manner of sounds enter the ear-root. From this condition, the ear-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"The nose-root, through conditions, is likewise attached to fragrances and contacts. The characteristics of fragrance cling and entangle, their principles manifold, and all manner of fragrances enter the nose-root. From this condition, the nose-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"The tongue-root, through conditions, is likewise attached to flavors and contacts. The characteristics of flavor cling and entangle, their principles manifold, and all manner of flavors enter the tongue-root. From this condition, the tongue-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"The body-root, through conditions, is likewise attached to textures—fine and smooth—and contacts. The characteristics of the body cling and entangle, their principles manifold, and all manner of textures enter the body-root. From this condition, the body-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"The mind-root, through conditions, is likewise attached to clinging and entanglements. Due to clinging, its principles are manifold, and all manner of clinging enters the mind-root. From this condition, the mind-root generates outflows. Once outflows arise and interact, they give birth to measureless and boundless dharmas, some good, some evil. From this indeterminate cause, evil fruits arise."

"Zuisheng, you must know that the arising of the three evil destinies is due to the six roots, six contacts, six feelings, and six defilements. These four great knots give rise to the various karmic causes of good and evil, amplify the manifold sufferings of the three evil destinies, and can alter the originally pure and good foundation from beginningless time. For this reason, all beings initially plant the seeds of their afflictions through the six roots. Once afflictions arise, they condition further arising without cessation."

"You must all understand that the six roots are a calamity beyond measure. Therefore, those who study the Dao must guard and discipline the six roots, never allowing them to wander in careless attachment to phenomena."

When these words were spoken, another great perfected one in the assembly, named Jueli, rose from his seat and approached the Heavenly Venerable. Prostrating himself with his head to the ground, he bowed and said: "Heavenly Venerable, you have just declared that at the inception of consciousness, all beings are purely good and undefiled, their actions necessarily in accord with principle, their movements in harmony with truth. In their advancing and retreating, rising and reclining, there is no lapse of propriety. Every dharma conforms to the essence of the Dao, without distinction. If this is so, how then do beings, in what they see and hear, in their movements and actions, become stained by coarse and defiled matters?"

"Venerable One, it is like a hall of ice, its beams and pillars, its mats and curtains, all formed of frozen ice. How, within such a place, could fire arise? How could the afflictions of heat and vexation come to be?"

At the inception of consciousness, all beings are likewise thus—pure in their mutual goodness, undivided and unadulterated. How then, through the six senses, do they become defiled? How do they cultivate evil and incur the fruits of sin?

Moreover, Venerable One, it is like lapis lazuli, which shines through within and without, its surface and interior entirely free of stain or blemish. The inception of consciousness is also thus: inherently good, and what it cultivates is likewise good. How then does it reap the manifold fruits of evil?

Again, Venerable One, it is like a casket of a hundred blended incenses, from which fragrance overflows, without a trace of foul or putrid odor. The inception of consciousness is also thus: inherently pure and fragrant, with no place for filth or impurity.

Again, Venerable One, it is like the radiant brilliance of sunlight, within which there is no darkness. The inception of consciousness is also thus: inherently luminous and clear. How then do brightness and obscurity come to be used together?

I now harbor this doubt: in the world, beings do not reject goodness yet commit boundless evils. I only pray that the Great Virtue will resolve this for me, that all in this assembly may be thoroughly cleansed and freed from all doubts and obstructions.

Thereupon, the Heavenly Venerable answered serenely: "Decisive Principle Perfected One, such a pointed question is beyond reckoning. You have all once drawn near to boundless and innumerable Perfected Ones, made offerings to boundless and innumerable Heavenly Venerables, attained great wisdom, and gained unhindered clarity. Thus are you able to utter these subtle and wondrous words. I shall now explain the truth of the matter in full. Listen carefully and do not grow weary.

Decisive Principle Great Perfected One, the nature of the human mind cannot be wholly likened to ice, lapis lazuli, or incense. The reason is this: such things are all static phenomena, their substance tranquil and unchanging, incapable of transformation, and thus their essence cannot generate anything else. Because they are static causes, external things cannot enter them. These analogies are therefore flawed. The analogy of the sun, however, may be apt. The reason is this: the sun is not perpetually bright, its body not perpetually upright; at times it is eclipsed, at times it declines and vanishes. The nature of the mind is likewise: shifting and unstable, driven by defilements, coveting self-interest, delighting in one’s own well-being, the eye coveting beautiful forms, the ear coveting beautiful sounds, the nose coveting beautiful fragrances, the tongue coveting delicious tastes, the body coveting pleasant touches, and the mind coveting ease and pleasure—all these are suffering. Whatever is coveted becomes the greatest suffering. Why is this so?

The eye covets beautiful forms, and each person’s mind generates manifold thoughts. If one constantly attains them, it harms the mind and thought, knowing only covetousness and love while forgetting the original goodness. If one does not constantly attain them, manifold desires and calculations arise. Seeing others with beautiful forms, one wishes to possess them all. Such beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions—how much more so when their desires are not immediately fulfilled.

The ear covets beautiful sounds, and each person’s mind generates manifold thoughts. If one constantly attains them, it disturbs the mind and thought, knowing only covetousness and love while forgetting the original goodness. If one does not constantly attain them, manifold desires and calculations arise. Hearing others with beautiful voices, one wishes to hear them all. Such beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions—how much more so when their desires are not immediately fulfilled.

The nose covets beautiful fragrances, and each person’s mind generates manifold thoughts. If one constantly attains them, it corrupts the mind and thought, knowing only covetousness and love while forgetting the original goodness. If one does not constantly attain them, manifold desires and calculations arise. Smelling others’ beautiful fragrances, one wishes to inhale them all. Such beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions.

The tongue covets delicious tastes, and each person’s mind generates manifold thoughts. If one constantly attains them, it troubles the mind and thought, knowing only covetousness and love while losing the original goodness. If one does not constantly attain them, manifold desires and calculations arise. Tasting others’ delicious flavors, one wishes to consume them all. Such beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions.

The body covets pleasant touches, clinging to softness and smoothness, and each person’s mind generates manifold thoughts. If one constantly attains them, it vexes the mind and thought, knowing only the movement of the mind, habituated to love and softness, while losing the original goodness. If one does not constantly attain them, manifold desires and calculations arise. Seeing others with fine textures, one wishes to attach them to oneself. Such beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions.

The mind is unstable, clinging to manifold thoughts. All conditions enter the mind, which then clings to the senses, ceaselessly coming and going. If all accords with the mind, arrogance and indulgence arise; if it opposes the mind, anger and resentment arise. Through these causes and conditions, beings directly bring upon themselves boundless afflictions.

Decisive Principle, know this: all in the world who remain tranquil and at ease, who do not cultivate superior practices, do not contemplate the true Dao, do not practice the six penetrations of supreme wisdom, do not delight in diligence, and do not plant the seeds of blessings—indulging instead in the six senses and led by the six emotions—naturally fall into the currents of the three evil destinies. The reason is this: the six senses and six defilements can generate boundless and manifold causes of sin, beyond reckoning. Thus, it is not that the inception of consciousness cultivates wickedness and incurs the fruits of sin; nor is it that seeing evil generates the fruits of sin; nor is it that hearing evil generates the fruits of sin. It is simply that the six defilements themselves give rise to all transgressions. Gradually growing, they lead to the manifold causes of hell, animal rebirth, and hungry ghosts.

Decisive Principle, know this: in a past age, there was a land called Sun-Earth. This kingdom was prosperous and joyful, like the heavens of the desire realm. Its people were boundless, all living in peace and happiness, constantly cultivating the wondrous Dao, and none failed to recite the teachings of the Three Caverns. In this land there was a king named Crown-Restrainer. This king was enlightened, possessed of great spiritual power, and in his walking, sitting, rising, and reclining, he displayed manifold transformations."

Suddenly, before the throne, a lotus flower appeared, its thousand petals lush and resplendent, its radiance dazzling. King Crown-Restrainer rose in astonishment, bowed to the flower, and in an instant, from within its petals emerged a boy, aged five or six, of unparalleled beauty, without equal in the Three Realms. Seeing this, the king immediately commanded that he be taken in and raised, naming him Transformation-Born. As he grew to maturity, his wisdom waxed like the moon, his spiritual powers were unhindered, and none in the boundless lands failed to submit to him. Whatever he sought or desired was fully granted. King Crown-Restrainer, revering his towering virtue and power, abdicated his throne and entrusted the kingdom to Transformation-Born. All gods and humans rejoiced, honoring and esteeming him without weariness.

One day, he assembled his ministers, kinsmen, state preceptors, Daoist masters, and scholars of the profound and the classical for a grand feast and discourse that lasted from dawn to dusk. Transformation-Born, the great king, raised his voice and declared: "In this land of ours, all is at ease, and we enjoy peace and happiness without decline. Yet I have heard of a land in the east called the Great Hall Village, the World of Received Goodness. Though I have heard its name, I have not seen its form. I wish to journey there with you all, to observe its customs and its people. Would this be acceptable?"

All the ministers replied in unison: "Great King, your spiritual powers are unhindered—what doubt could there be in your desire to survey all? We, too, wish to rely on your majestic might and witness the wonders of that land together."

At that moment, without rising from his seat, he employed his spiritual power and arrived there in an instant, accompanied by his ministers and his palace. Upon reaching that land, the king of Received Goodness ordered his chariot to go forth and welcome them. From afar, he bowed in reverence, inquired after their well-being, and together they returned to the city, where a great high seat was prepared. This seat was vast and boundless, beyond the reckoning of the world. Transformation-Born ascended and sat upon it, emitting radiant light and performing manifold divine transformations. Throughout the land, he manifested four hundred million jeweled banners, each forty thousand *li* in height. Atop each banner shone a great light, illuminating all with such brilliance that the radiance of the sun and moon was eclipsed and rendered useless. Seeing this light, all the people marveled, saying to one another: "How is it that such a wondrous sign has suddenly appeared in our land?" They gathered in groups of five or three, discussing and debating, yet none could fully explain it.

Then Transformation-Born thundered with a great voice, proclaiming to the people: "You may ride this light and enter the royal city." As soon as these words were spoken, all within the kingdom heard them. Following the light, each arrived at the king’s presence. There they saw Transformation-Born seated upon the high seat, and all bowed their heads in reverence, standing with respect. Once the assembly was complete, Transformation-Born raised his voice and declared: "Let all here know that the nature of all dharmas cannot be eternally preserved—they must decay and pass away according to conditions. Do not think this land’s happiness is secure, for there are countless lands more excellent than this. If one cultivates good karma, one is certain to be reborn there." He then bestowed upon the people the Ten Good Precepts: not to kill, not to steal, not to engage in sexual misconduct, not to consume intoxicants, not to speak falsely, not to use divisive speech, not to engage in double-tongued speech, not to use harsh speech, not to covet, and not to harbor anger. He declared: "If you firmly uphold these ten precepts, know that you will certainly be reborn in a wondrous land."

After speaking thus, the multitude took refuge and all received and upheld the precepts, never faltering for the rest of their lives. The king of Received Goodness, too, attained spiritual powers and a purified, wondrously transformed mind. When the teaching was complete, Transformation-Born rose lightly and returned home. Along the way, he thoroughly examined every land he passed. Upon returning to his own realm, he asked a minister named Penetrating Vision: "When you accompanied me to the World of Received Goodness, what did you observe? Speak of it in full."

Penetrating Vision replied: "Great King, as I attended you, I surveyed that land and saw that its earth was as flat as a palm, without sand or gravel. Various fruits were planted in orderly rows, straight as a line or a knife’s cut. All the people ate the fruits of the trees, their forms upright and comely, with little sickness or suffering. The city walls and palaces were predominantly blue, like morning clouds resting upon green mountains. Upon reflection, this land cannot compare to ours."

Transformation-Born Great King addressed Penetrating Vision: "What else did you observe?"

Penetrating Vision replied: "I further saw jeweled pearl-banners—these were manifestations of Your Majesty’s power, not the land’s true native endowment. If it were only thus, it would not be worthy of aspiration."

Transformation-Born Great King answered his ministers: "Each of you, rest and reflect. We shall journey more widely yet." The ministers, having heard this, withdrew to their places and sat in silent reverence, hands clasped.

*Thus concludes Fascicle One of the* **Supreme One-Vehicle Ocean-Empty Wisdom Treasury Sutra**.

---

**Fascicle Two: The Lamentation Chapter**

At that time, Ocean-Empty Wisdom Treasury rose from his seat, bowed his head in homage, knelt with hands clasped, and addressed the Heavenly Worthy: "What has been spoken is truly profound, its subtle virtue and power beyond conception. I now reflect upon the origins of the myriad transformations of the ten thousand kinds. How is it that all beings and all dharmas each possess their Dao-nature, arising from causes and conditions? Given these causes and conditions, should there be bondage, or should there be no bondage? Are these the five aggregates, or the six sense-objects? As I contemplate this, thought after thought arises and perishes. In such arising and perishing, who is bound, and who is not bound? Who is liberated, and who is not liberated? Through these causes and conditions, one set of five aggregates gives rise to another; with the cessation of these aggregates, those aggregates do not arise. How can the five aggregates be defiled by afflictions, when they arise and perish in each moment? What, then, are afflictions?"

Then the Heavenly Worthy answered Ocean-Empty: "Excellent, excellent, good man! Listen carefully, listen carefully, and I shall distinguish and explain this for you. Good man, it is like when beings approach the moment of death, great sorrow and suffering arise. Parents, spouse, and kin gather around, wailing in anguish. They gaze in terror, not knowing where to turn for refuge. Though the five senses remain, there is no awareness; the limbs tremble uncontrollably, the body grows cold and hollow, and the breath of life nears its end. Then they see the retribution of the sins and merits they have cultivated, following their karma to be reborn—whether in the three evil paths, the five destinies, or among humans—each according to their deeds, without exception. Good man, it is like the setting sun: as it nears its descent, the shadows of mountains and hills appear to shift eastward, though in truth it does not set in the west. The fruition of beings’ karma is likewise. These five aggregates perish, and those five aggregates arise. It is like a lamp: when the lamp is lit, darkness vanishes; when the lamp is extinguished, darkness returns. Good man, it is like a clay vessel: clay and vessel unite, and when the clay is transformed, the vessel is formed. Yet the nature of the clay is never called a vessel. Though the vessel is not clay, it does not come from elsewhere—it arises from the causes and conditions of clay. Good man, these five aggregates perish, and those five aggregates arise. These aggregates do not transform into those aggregates, nor do those aggregates arise of themselves or from elsewhere. It is through these aggregates that those aggregates arise. It is like the clay vessel: when the clay is transformed, the vessel is formed. Though their substance is not different, they differ according to time. For this reason, through these five aggregates, those five aggregates arise. With the cessation of these aggregates, I speak of those aggregates. Whether virtuous or unvirtuous aggregates, observing good and evil, when parents unite in the mingling of vital essences, one is reborn according to karmic causes and conditions. Love arises toward the mother, affection toward the father; when parents think of the child, joy fills their hearts. From such conditions arise inverted causes: what is unloved is seen as loved, what is suffering as joy, what is impermanent as permanent. Such inverted views give rise to good and evil causes, and afflictions produce karma. Karma produces afflictions—this is called the bond of entanglement. For this reason, there is arising and perishing in each moment. If a virtuous person draws near to a wise teacher, they may hear and behold the wondrous scriptures of the Three Caverns and the Ten Divisions of the *Numinous Treasure*. Having heard the Dharma, they pay reverence, circumambulate, and praise it; they recite, transcribe, and single-mindedly uphold it, making pure offerings. Six times a day, they contemplate and reflect upon it, never ceasing. At such times, they sit in repentance. Once the mind of repentance arises, joy follows. With a joyful mind, they abandon their spouse and children, dwell alone in the mountains and forests, and preserve the true mindfulness of the Dao. Through these causes and conditions, they attain liberation within the cycle of birth and death, extinguishing all afflictions. Like a lamp dispelling darkness, this is called *nirvāṇa*. For this reason, it is called the cessation of causes and conditions."

Then among the assembly, a youth named Good Talent rose from his seat, approached the Heavenly Worthy with measured steps, bowed his head in homage, and said: "Heavenly Worthy, what you have expounded today is of profound and subtle virtue and power, not something the lesser vehicles can fathom. How then can you speak of these five aggregates perishing and those five aggregates arising, yet say that these aggregates do not transform into those aggregates? How can such five aggregates be bound by afflictions? If there are afflictions, there should be no arising and perishing; if there is arising and perishing, how can afflictions arise once they have ceased, given that the five aggregates are based on causes and conditions? I beseech the Heavenly Worthy to distinguish and explain this, so that all in this assembly may resolve their doubts."

Then the Heavenly Worthy answered Good Talent: "Excellent, excellent, good man! Let all of you in this assembly listen carefully, and I shall distinguish and explain how afflictions strike and bind. Good man, afflictions strike and bind the five aggregates; apart from the five aggregates, there are no separate afflictions; apart from afflictions, there are no separate five aggregates. Good man, it is like a wooden bed: apart from wood, there is no bed; apart from the bed, there is no wood."

For this reason, the five aggregates of sentient beings are likewise: when there are afflictions, they are called "bound"; when there are no afflictions, they are called "liberated." Good man, it is like the eye-root: when open, it sees forms; when closed, it sees nothing. The striking and binding of afflictions, their arising and perishing, are in principle no different. The five aggregates of sentient beings are likewise: if there are afflictions, they are called "struck and bound"; if there are no afflictions, this is called "liberation." For this reason, it is said that afflictions strike and bind sentient beings; if afflictions cease, then there are no sentient beings. Apart from afflictions, there are no separate sentient beings; apart from sentient beings, there are no separate afflictions. It is also called "afflictions striking and binding sentient beings," and it is also called "sentient beings striking and binding afflictions."

Good Talent then said: "If this is so, the eye does not see itself, the finger does not point to itself, the blade does not cut itself, and the recipient does not receive itself. How then can the Heavenly Worthy say that afflictions bind afflictions? Why is this? These afflictions are none other than sentient beings; these sentient beings are none other than afflictions. If it is said that afflictions strike and bind sentient beings, it should be known that afflictions strike and bind afflictions."

Then the Heavenly Worthy answered Good Talent: "Good man, it is like when the eye closes—there is no other dharma that comes to close it. Afflictions and sentient beings are likewise. For this reason, I say that afflictions bind sentient beings. If there are sentient beings free from all afflictions, then they attain liberation. Therefore, I say that sentient beings are liberated."

Good Talent then said: "If afflictions are what strike and bind, how can the Heavenly Worthy speak of the divine sages dwelling in the city of afflictions, yet not be struck and bound?"

Then the Heavenly Worthy answered Good Talent: "Good man, dwelling has two kinds: first, transformative dwelling; second, fruitional dwelling. As for transformative dwelling, the Heavenly Worthy and sages dwell in afflictions, yet in truth have no afflictions. Why is this? They have realized the nature of afflictions. For the sake of sentient beings, they dwell by expedient means. What is fruitional dwelling? Good man, these sentient beings have not yet been able to relinquish the binding of afflictions. Because of this binding, they revolve in birth and death and do not perceive the nature of the Dao. Because they do not perceive it, they do not attain the vision of my eternal and blissful mind. For this reason, they constantly abide in the three realms, struck and bound by afflictions. For this reason, being struck and bound by afflictions is called 'sentient beings.' This body of sentient beings is none other than afflictions."

Then Good Talent addressed the Heavenly Worthy: "The five aggregates of sentient beings are empty and without substance. Given this, who is there to receive teachings and cultivate virtue and the Dao?"

The Heavenly Worthy replied: "Good man, all sentient beings possess the mind of thought. The initial thought-moment arises with diligent progress and generates the mind of generosity. Such dharmas as these arise thought-moment after thought-moment. For this reason, they continue without interruption, and this is called 'cultivating the Dao.'"

Good Talent then said: "As you have explained, thought-moments arise successively—each thought-moment arises, and once it has arisen, the next thought-moment has not yet come to be. Given this, how can they continue without interruption and be called 'cultivating the Dao'?"

Then the Heavenly Worthy answered Good Talent: "It is just so, exactly as you have said. Good man, it is like a lamp’s flame: though it perishes thought-moment after thought-moment, it still produces light that effectively dispels darkness. All dharmas such as thoughts are likewise. Good man, it is like the actions of sentient beings: though they perish thought-moment after thought-moment, they still reach their destinations, moving east or west as they wish. It is like taking medicine: though it perishes thought-moment after thought-moment, it can still cure illness. It is like the sun and moon: though they perish thought-moment after thought-moment, they can still cause trees, forests, and grasses to grow. Good Talent, as you have said, once a thought-moment has passed, how can there be continuity? Good man, it is like a person singing: the voice may be loud or soft, and the five notes cannot all be produced at once. The first does not reach the middle, the middle does not reach the last, and both loud and soft perish thought-moment after thought-moment. Because of this perishing of thought-moments, over time a wondrous sound is formed. The cultivation of the Dao by people is likewise. Good man, it is like a person counting: one does not reach two, two does not reach three, and though they perish thought-moment after thought-moment, they still reach a thousand or ten thousand. The cultivation of the Dao by people is likewise. Good man, it is like a lamp’s flame: the flame perishes thought-moment after thought-moment. Once the initial blaze has perished, the later blaze does not say, 'I perish, and you arise.' Knowing this arising and perishing, the nature of the lamp is thus formed."

Good Talent then said: "As you have explained, even after long cultivation of the Dao and attaining the four heavens, one must still diligently cultivate kindness and compassion, refrain from killing, sexual misconduct, and stealing, guard the mind against jealousy, avoid divisive speech, and abstain from intoxicants and meat—yet still one cannot attain the pure and wondrous realm of the sovereign. How then can it be said that through long cultivation of precepts amidst evil afflictions one attains the Dao?"

The Heavenly Worthy replied: "All sentient beings, though amidst afflictions, long cultivate the precepts. Good man, all sentient beings do not lose the nature of the Dao. Though born in evil realms, by the power of the Dao they attain the cultivation of wondrous realms. Good man, it is like a treasure mountain within a noxious forest, where a fierce tiger constantly dwells. For this reason, all sentient beings—birds and beasts—avoid this mountain, none daring to approach. A poor man reflects and thinks, 'How can I go to that mountain to gather treasures, so that today I may quickly become wealthy?' Thereupon, he practices various poisons and goes to the mountain. Then, the evil creatures, upon smelling his poisons, immediately depart. Thus, the poor man gathers treasures as he wishes. The long cultivation of precepts amidst afflictions, culminating in the attainment of the Dao, is likewise.
